<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Livestock_production_systems">Livestock production systems</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">Livestock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">Animal husbandry</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_domesticated_animals" title="List of domesticated animals">List of domesticated animals</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="1079" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/220px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/330px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg/440px-Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hog_confinement_barn_interior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Intensive_animal_farming" title="Intensive animal farming">Intensively farmed</a> pigs</div></div></div>
<p>Animal husbandry is the breeding and raising of animals for meat, milk, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">eggs</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a>, and for work and transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clutton-Brock_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clutton-Brock-124">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Working_animal" title="Working animal">Working animals</a>, including horses, <a href="/wiki/Mule" title="Mule">mules</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">oxen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a>, camels, llamas, alpacas, donkeys, and dogs, have for centuries been used to help cultivate fields, <a href="/wiki/Harvest" title="Harvest">harvest</a> crops, wrangle other animals, and transport farm products to buyers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Livestock production systems can be defined based on feed source, as grassland-based, mixed, and landless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_lps_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_lps-126">[126]</a></sup> As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 30% of Earth's ice- and water-free area was used for producing livestock, with the sector employing approximately 1.3 billion people. Between the 1960s and the 2000s, there was a significant increase in livestock production, both by numbers and by carcass weight, especially among beef, pigs and chickens, the latter of which had production increased by almost a factor of 10. Non-meat animals, such as milk cows and egg-producing chickens, also showed significant production increases. Global cattle, sheep and goat populations are expected to continue to increase sharply through 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LP_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-LP-127">[127]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</a> or fish farming, the production of fish for human consumption in confined operations, is one of the fastest growing sectors of food production, growing at an average of 9% a year between 1975 and 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the second half of the 20th century, producers using selective breeding focused on creating livestock <a href="/wiki/Breed" title="Breed">breeds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Crossbreed" title="Crossbreed">crossbreeds</a> that increased production, while mostly disregarding the need to preserve <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a>. This trend has led to a significant decrease in genetic diversity and resources among livestock breeds, leading to a corresponding decrease in disease resistance and local adaptations previously found among traditional breeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Broiler_house.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Broiler_house.jpg/220px-Broiler_house.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Broiler_house.jpg/330px-Broiler_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Broiler_house.jpg/440px-Broiler_house.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Broiler_house.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Raising chickens intensively for meat in a broiler house</div></div></div>
<p>Grassland based livestock production relies upon plant material such as <a href="/wiki/Shrubland" title="Shrubland">shrubland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rangeland" title="Rangeland">rangeland</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Managed_intensive_rotational_grazing" title="Managed intensive rotational grazing">pastures</a> for feeding <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">ruminant</a> animals. Outside nutrient inputs may be used, however manure is returned directly to the grassland as a major nutrient source. This system is particularly important in areas where crop production is not feasible because of climate or soil, representing 30–40 million pastoralists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_121-4"><a href="#cite_note-CS-121">[121]</a></sup> Mixed production systems use grassland, <a href="/wiki/Fodder" title="Fodder">fodder</a> crops and grain feed crops as feed for ruminant and monogastric (one stomach; mainly chickens and pigs) livestock. Manure is typically recycled in mixed systems as a fertilizer for crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_lps_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_lps-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Landless systems rely upon feed from outside the farm, representing the de-linking of crop and livestock production found more prevalently in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a> member countries. Synthetic fertilizers are more heavily relied upon for crop production and manure use becomes a challenge as well as a source for pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_lps_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_lps-126">[126]</a></sup> Industrialized countries use these operations to produce much of the global supplies of poultry and pork. Scientists estimate that 75% of the growth in livestock production between 2003 and 2030 will be in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confined_animal_feeding_operations" title="Confined animal feeding operations">confined animal feeding operations</a>, sometimes called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Factory_farming" title="Factory farming">factory farming</a>. Much of this growth is happening in developing countries in Asia, with much smaller amounts of growth in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LP_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-LP-127">[127]</a></sup> Some of the practices used in commercial livestock production, including the usage of <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormones</a>, are controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production_practices">Production practices</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fendt_Tractor_Ripping_up_Kulin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Fendt_Tractor_Ripping_up_Kulin.jpg/220px-Fendt_Tractor_Ripping_up_Kulin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Fendt_Tractor_Ripping_up_Kulin.jpg/330px-Fendt_Tractor_Ripping_up_Kulin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Fendt_Tractor_Ripping_up_Kulin.jpg/440px-Fendt_Tractor_Ripping_up_Kulin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fendt_Tractor_Ripping_up_Kulin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">Tilling</a> an arable field</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">Tillage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crop_rotation" title="Crop rotation">Crop rotation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">Irrigation</a></div>
<p>Tillage is the practice of breaking up the soil with tools such as the plow or <a href="/wiki/Harrow_(tool)" title="Harrow (tool)">harrow</a> to prepare for planting, for nutrient incorporation, or for pest control. Tillage varies in intensity from conventional to <a href="/wiki/No-till_farming" title="No-till farming">no-till</a>. It may improve productivity by warming the soil, incorporating fertilizer and controlling weeds, but also renders soil more prone to erosion, triggers the decomposition of organic matter releasing CO<sub>2</sub>, and reduces the abundance and diversity of soil organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soil_nutrient_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soil_nutrient-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCP_Tillage_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_Tillage-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pest control includes the management of weeds, insects, <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a>, and diseases. Chemical (pesticides), biological (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biocontrol" title="Biocontrol">biocontrol</a>), mechanical (tillage), and cultural practices are used. Cultural practices include crop rotation, <a href="/wiki/Culling" title="Culling">culling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cover_crop" title="Cover crop">cover crops</a>, intercropping, <a href="/wiki/Compost" title="Compost">composting</a>, avoidance, and <a href="/wiki/Disease_resistance_in_fruit_and_vegetables" title="Disease resistance in fruit and vegetables">resistance</a>. Integrated pest management attempts to use all of these methods to keep pest populations below the number which would cause economic loss, and recommends pesticides as a last resort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCP_Pest_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_Pest-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nutrient_management" title="Nutrient management">Nutrient management</a> includes both the source of nutrient inputs for crop and livestock production, and the method of use of manure produced by livestock. Nutrient inputs can be chemical inorganic fertilizers, manure, <a href="/wiki/Green_manure" title="Green manure">green manure</a>, compost and minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCP_Soil_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_Soil-134">[134]</a></sup> Crop nutrient use may also be managed using cultural techniques such as crop rotation or a <a href="/wiki/Fallow" title="Fallow">fallow</a> period. Manure is used either by holding livestock where the feed crop is growing, such as in managed intensive rotational grazing, or <a href="/wiki/Manure_spreader" title="Manure spreader">by spreading</a> either dry or liquid formulations of manure on cropland or <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pastures</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soil_nutrient_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-Soil_nutrient-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_nutrient_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-CS_nutrient-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PivotWithDrops.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/PivotWithDrops.JPG/220px-PivotWithDrops.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/PivotWithDrops.JPG/330px-PivotWithDrops.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/PivotWithDrops.JPG/440px-PivotWithDrops.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PivotWithDrops.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center_pivot_irrigation" title="Center pivot irrigation">center pivot irrigation</a> system</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_management" title="Water management">Water management</a> is needed where rainfall is insufficient or variable, which occurs to some degree in most regions of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_121-5"><a href="#cite_note-CS-121">[121]</a></sup> Some farmers use irrigation to supplement rainfall. In other areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Plains" title="Great Plains">Great Plains</a> in the U.S. and Canada, farmers use a fallow year to conserve soil moisture to use for growing a crop in the following year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PCP_Water_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-PCP_Water-136">[136]</a></sup> Agriculture represents 70% of freshwater use worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pimentel_water_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pimentel_water-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to a report by the <a href="/wiki/International_Food_Policy_Research_Institute" title="International Food Policy Research Institute">International Food Policy Research Institute</a>, agricultural technologies will have the greatest impact on food production if adopted in combination with each other; using a model that assessed how eleven technologies could impact agricultural productivity, food security and trade by 2050, the International Food Policy Research Institute found that the number of people at risk from hunger could be reduced by as much as 40% and food prices could be reduced by almost half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifpri_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-ifpri-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Payment_for_ecosystem_services" title="Payment for ecosystem services">Payment for ecosystem services</a> is a method of providing additional incentives to encourage farmers to conserve some aspects of the environment. Measures might include paying for reforestation upstream of a city, to improve the supply of fresh water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_climate_change_on_yields">Effects of climate change on yields</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture" title="Effects of climate change on agriculture">Effects of climate change on agriculture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Winnowing_The_Grain,_Axum,_Ethiopia_(Detail)_(3157508890).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1222" data-file-width="1505" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Winnowing_The_Grain%2C_Axum%2C_Ethiopia_%28Detail%29_%283157508890%29.jpg/220px-Winnowing_The_Grain%2C_Axum%2C_Ethiopia_%28Detail%29_%283157508890%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Winnowing_The_Grain%2C_Axum%2C_Ethiopia_%28Detail%29_%283157508890%29.jpg/330px-Winnowing_The_Grain%2C_Axum%2C_Ethiopia_%28Detail%29_%283157508890%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Winnowing_The_Grain%2C_Axum%2C_Ethiopia_%28Detail%29_%283157508890%29.jpg/440px-Winnowing_The_Grain%2C_Axum%2C_Ethiopia_%28Detail%29_%283157508890%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Winnowing_The_Grain,_Axum,_Ethiopia_(Detail)_(3157508890).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Winnowing" title="Winnowing">Winnowing</a> grain: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> will probably harm crop yields in low latitude countries like Ethiopia.</div></div></div><p><a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">Climate change</a> and agriculture are interrelated on a global scale. <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture" title="Effects of climate change on agriculture">Global warming affects agriculture</a> through changes in <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_temperature_record" title="Instrumental temperature record">average temperatures</a>, rainfall, and <a href="/wiki/Extreme_weather" title="Extreme weather">weather extremes</a> (like storms and heat waves); changes in pests and diseases; changes in atmospheric <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and ground-level <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> concentrations; changes in the nutritional quality of some foods;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science-news_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-science-news-140">[140]</a></sup> and changes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Current_sea_level_rise" title="Current sea level rise">sea level</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Global warming is already affecting agriculture, with effects unevenly distributed across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_summary_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-porter_summary-142">[142]</a></sup> Future climate change will probably negatively affect <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop production</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_latitude" title="Low latitude">low latitude</a> countries, while effects in northern <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a> may be positive or negative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_summary_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-porter_summary-142">[142]</a></sup> Global warming will probably increase the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_insecurity" title="Food insecurity">food insecurity</a> for some vulnerable groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crop_alteration_and_biotechnology">Crop alteration and biotechnology</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant_breeding">Plant breeding</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">Plant breeding</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="189" data-file-width="252" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg/220px-Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wheat_selection_k10183-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wheat cultivar tolerant of high <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> (left) compared with non-tolerant variety</div></div></div>
<p>Crop alteration has been practiced by humankind for thousands of years, since the beginning of civilization. Altering crops through breeding practices changes the genetic make-up of a plant to develop crops with more beneficial characteristics for humans, for example, larger fruits or seeds, drought-tolerance, or resistance to pests. Significant advances in plant breeding ensued after the work of geneticist <a href="/wiki/Gregor_Mendel" title="Gregor Mendel">Gregor Mendel</a>. His work on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dominant_allele" title="Dominant allele">dominant</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recessive_allele" title="Recessive allele">recessive alleles</a>, although initially largely ignored for almost 50 years, gave plant breeders a better understanding of genetics and breeding techniques. Crop breeding includes techniques such as plant selection with desirable traits, <a href="/wiki/Self-pollination" title="Self-pollination">self-pollination</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-pollination" title="Cross-pollination">cross-pollination</a>, and molecular techniques that genetically modify the organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>Domestication of plants has, over the centuries increased yield, improved disease resistance and <a href="/wiki/Drought_tolerance" title="Drought tolerance">drought tolerance</a>, eased harvest and improved the taste and nutritional value of crop plants. Careful selection and breeding have had enormous effects on the characteristics of crop plants. Plant selection and breeding in the 1920s and 1930s improved pasture (grasses and clover) in New Zealand. Extensive X-ray and ultraviolet induced mutagenesis efforts (i.e. primitive genetic engineering) during the 1950s produced the modern commercial varieties of grains such as wheat, corn (maize) and barley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seedlings_in_Green_House.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Seedlings_in_Green_House.jpg/220px-Seedlings_in_Green_House.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Seedlings_in_Green_House.jpg/330px-Seedlings_in_Green_House.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Seedlings_in_Green_House.jpg/440px-Seedlings_in_Green_House.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seedlings_in_Green_House.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seedlings in a green house. This is what it looks like when seedlings are growing from plant breeding.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> popularized the use of conventional <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a> to sharply increase yield by creating "high-yielding varieties". For example, average yields of corn (maize) in the US have increased from around 2.5 tons per hectare (t/ha) (40 bushels per acre) in 1900 to about 9.4 t/ha (150 bushels per acre) in 2001. Similarly, worldwide average wheat yields have increased from less than 1 t/ha in 1900 to more than 2.5 t/ha in 1990. South American average wheat yields are around 2 t/ha, African under 1 t/ha, and Egypt and Arabia up to 3.5 to 4 t/ha with irrigation. In contrast, the average wheat yield in countries such as France is over 8 t/ha. Variations in yields are due mainly to variation in climate, genetics, and the level of intensive farming techniques (use of fertilizers, chemical pest control, growth control to avoid lodging).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_engineering">Genetic engineering</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">Genetic engineering</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">Genetically modified food</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">Genetically modified crops</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Regulation_of_the_release_of_genetic_modified_organisms" title="Regulation of the release of genetic modified organisms">Regulation of the release of genetic modified organisms</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food_controversies" title="Genetically modified food controversies">Genetically modified food controversies</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_382_Genetically_Modified_Potatoes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1692" data-file-width="2554" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CSIRO_ScienceImage_382_Genetically_Modified_Potatoes.jpg/220px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_382_Genetically_Modified_Potatoes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CSIRO_ScienceImage_382_Genetically_Modified_Potatoes.jpg/330px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_382_Genetically_Modified_Potatoes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CSIRO_ScienceImage_382_Genetically_Modified_Potatoes.jpg/440px-CSIRO_ScienceImage_382_Genetically_Modified_Potatoes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CSIRO_ScienceImage_382_Genetically_Modified_Potatoes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">Genetically modified</a> potato plants (left) resist virus diseases that damage unmodified plants (right).</div></div></div>
<p>Genetically modified organisms (GMO) are <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> whose <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> material has been altered by genetic engineering techniques generally known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA_technology" title="Recombinant DNA technology">recombinant DNA technology</a>. Genetic engineering has expanded the genes available to breeders to use in creating desired germlines for new crops. Increased durability, nutritional content, insect and virus resistance and herbicide tolerance are a few of the attributes bred into crops through genetic engineering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> For some, GMO crops cause <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">food safety</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_labeling_regulations" title="Food labeling regulations">food labeling</a> concerns. Numerous countries have placed restrictions on the production, import or use of GMO foods and crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Currently a global treaty, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biosafety_Protocol" title="Biosafety Protocol">Biosafety Protocol</a>, regulates the trade of GMOs. There is ongoing discussion regarding the labeling of foods made from GMOs, and while the EU currently requires all GMO foods to be labeled, the US does not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>Herbicide-resistant seed has a gene implanted into its genome that allows the plants to tolerate exposure to herbicides, including <a href="/wiki/Glyphosate" title="Glyphosate">glyphosate</a>. These seeds allow the farmer to grow a crop that can be sprayed with herbicides to control weeds without harming the resistant crop. Herbicide-tolerant crops are used by farmers worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> With the increasing use of herbicide-tolerant crops, comes an increase in the use of glyphosate-based herbicide sprays. In some areas glyphosate resistant weeds have developed, causing farmers to switch to other herbicides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Farmers_Guide_to_GMOs_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Farmers_Guide_to_GMOs-154">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Some studies also link widespread glyphosate usage to iron deficiencies in some crops, which is both a crop production and a nutritional quality concern, with potential economic and health implications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other GMO crops used by growers include insect-resistant crops, which have a gene from the soil bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_thuringiensis" title="Bacillus thuringiensis">Bacillus thuringiensis</a></i> (Bt), which produces a toxin specific to insects. These crops resist damage by insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> Some believe that similar or better pest-resistance traits can be acquired through traditional breeding practices, and resistance to various pests can be gained through hybridization or cross-pollination with wild species. In some cases, wild species are the primary source of resistance traits; some tomato cultivars that have gained resistance to at least 19 diseases did so through crossing with wild populations of tomatoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p><span class="anchor" id="Criticisms"></span>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_issues_with_agriculture" title="Environmental issues with agriculture">Environmental issues with agriculture</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_and_costs">Effects and costs</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG/170px-Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG/255px-Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG/340px-Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_pollution_in_the_Wairarapa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">Water pollution</a> in a rural stream due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agricultural_pollution_in_New_Zealand" title="Agricultural pollution in New Zealand">runoff from farming activity in New Zealand</a></div></div></div>
<p>Agriculture is both a cause of and sensitive to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_degradation" title="Soil degradation">soil degradation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_agriculture" title="Effects of climate change on agriculture">global warming</a>, which cause decrease in crop yield.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> Agriculture is one of the most important drivers of environmental pressures, particularly habitat change, climate change, water use and toxic emissions. Agriculture is the main source of toxins released into the environment, including insecticides, especially those used on cotton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (December 2021)">page needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The 2011 UNEP Green Economy report stated that agricultural operations produced some 13 per cent of anthropogenic global greenhouse gas emissions. This includes gases from the use of inorganic fertilizers, agro-chemical pesticides, and herbicides, as well as fossil fuel-energy inputs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unep.org_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Agriculture imposes multiple external costs upon society through effects such as pesticide damage to nature (especially herbicides and insecticides), nutrient runoff, excessive water usage, and loss of natural environment. A 2000 assessment of agriculture in the UK determined total external costs for 1996 of £2,343 million, or £208 per hectare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pretty2000_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pretty2000-163">[163]</a></sup> A 2005 analysis of these costs in the US concluded that cropland imposes approximately $5 to $16 billion ($30 to $96 per hectare), while livestock production imposes $714 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tegtmeier2005_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tegtmeier2005-164">[164]</a></sup> Both studies, which focused solely on the fiscal impacts, concluded that more should be done to internalize external costs. Neither included subsidies in their analysis, but they noted that subsidies also influence the cost of agriculture to society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pretty2000_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pretty2000-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tegtmeier2005_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tegtmeier2005-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>Agriculture seeks to increase yield and to reduce costs. Yield increases with inputs such as fertilisers and removal of pathogens, predators, and competitors (such as weeds). Costs decrease with increasing scale of farm units, such as making fields larger; this means removing <a href="/wiki/Hedge" title="Hedge">hedges</a>, ditches and other areas of habitat. Pesticides kill insects, plants and fungi. These and other measures have cut biodiversity to very low levels on intensively farmed land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Effective yields fall with on-farm losses, which may be caused by poor production practices during harvesting, handling, and storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Livestock_issues">Livestock issues</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biogas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1389" data-file-width="2046" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Biogas.jpg/220px-Biogas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Biogas.jpg/330px-Biogas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Biogas.jpg/440px-Biogas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biogas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Farmyard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digester" title="Anaerobic digester">anaerobic digester</a> converts waste plant material and manure from livestock into <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> fuel.</div></div></div>
<p>A senior UN official, Henning Steinfeld, said that "Livestock are one of the most significant contributors to today's most serious environmental problems".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Livestock production occupies 70% of all land used for agriculture, or 30% of the land surface of the planet. It is one of the largest sources of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>, responsible for 18% of the world's <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> as measured in CO<sub>2</sub> equivalents. By comparison, all transportation emits 13.5% of the CO<sub>2</sub>. It produces 65% of human-related <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (which has 296 times the global warming potential of CO<sub>2</sub>) and 37% of all human-induced <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (which is 23 times as warming as CO<sub>2</sub>.) It also generates 64% of the <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> emission. Livestock expansion is cited as a key factor driving <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>; in the Amazon basin 70% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deforestation_of_the_Amazon_Rainforest" title="Deforestation of the Amazon Rainforest">previously forested area</a> is now occupied by pastures and the remainder used for feed crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LEAD_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-LEAD-168">[168]</a></sup> Through deforestation and <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">land degradation</a>, livestock is also driving reductions in biodiversity. Furthermore, the UNEP states that "<a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane emissions</a> from global livestock are projected to increase by 60 per cent by 2030 under current practices and consumption patterns."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unep.org_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-unep.org-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_and_water_issues">Land and water issues</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_irrigation" title="Environmental impact of irrigation">Environmental impact of irrigation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crops_Kansas_AST_20010624.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2481" data-file-width="2589" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Crops_Kansas_AST_20010624.jpg/240px-Crops_Kansas_AST_20010624.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Crops_Kansas_AST_20010624.jpg/360px-Crops_Kansas_AST_20010624.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Crops_Kansas_AST_20010624.jpg/480px-Crops_Kansas_AST_20010624.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crops_Kansas_AST_20010624.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Circular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irrigated" title="Irrigated">irrigated</a> crop fields in <a href="/wiki/Haskell_County,_Kansas" title="Haskell County, Kansas">Kansas</a>. Healthy, growing crops of <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">sorghum</a> are green (sorghum may be slightly paler). Wheat is brilliant gold. Fields of brown have been recently harvested and plowed or have lain in <a href="/wiki/Fallow" title="Fallow">fallow</a> for the year.</div></div></div>
<p>Land transformation, the use of land to yield goods and services, is the most substantial way humans alter the Earth's ecosystems, and is the driving force causing <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a>. Estimates of the amount of land transformed by humans vary from 39 to 50%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitousek_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vitousek-169">[169]</a></sup> Land degradation, the long-term decline in ecosystem function and productivity, is estimated to be occurring on 24% of land worldwide, with cropland overrepresented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO_GLADA_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO_GLADA-170">[170]</a></sup> Land management is the driving factor behind degradation; 1.5 billion people rely upon the degrading land. Degradation can be through deforestation, <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, mineral depletion, <a href="/wiki/Soil_acidification" title="Soil acidification">acidification</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Soil_salinity" title="Soil salinity">salinization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_121-6"><a href="#cite_note-CS-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">Eutrophication</a>, excessive nutrient enrichment in <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">aquatic ecosystems</a> resulting in <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal blooms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anoxic_waters" title="Anoxic waters">anoxia</a>, leads to <a href="/wiki/Fish_kill" title="Fish kill">fish kills</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loss_of_biodiversity" title="Loss of biodiversity">loss of biodiversity</a>, and renders water unfit for drinking and other industrial uses. Excessive fertilization and manure application to cropland, as well as high livestock stocking densities cause nutrient (mainly <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>) <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(agriculture)" title="Leaching (agriculture)">leaching</a> from agricultural land. These nutrients are major <a href="/wiki/Nonpoint_source_pollution" title="Nonpoint source pollution">nonpoint pollutants</a> contributing to <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> of aquatic ecosystems and pollution of groundwater, with harmful effects on human populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eutr_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eutr-171">[171]</a></sup> Fertilisers also reduce terrestrial biodiversity by increasing competition for light, favouring those species that are able to benefit from the added nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hautier_Niklaus_Hector_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hautier_Niklaus_Hector-172">[172]</a></sup>
Agriculture accounts for 70 percent of withdrawals of freshwater resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Agriculture is a major draw on water from <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a>, and currently draws from those underground water sources at an unsustainable rate. It is long known that aquifers in areas as diverse as northern China, the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Upper Ganges</a> and the western US are being depleted, and new research extends these problems to aquifers in Iran, Mexico and Saudi Arabia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Increasing pressure is being placed on water resources by industry and urban areas, meaning that <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">water scarcity</a> is increasing and agriculture is facing the challenge of producing more food for the world's growing population with reduced water resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Farm_water" title="Farm water">Agricultural water</a> usage can also cause major environmental problems, including the destruction of natural wetlands, the spread of water-borne diseases, and land degradation through salinization and waterlogging, when irrigation is performed incorrectly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pesticides">Pesticides</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_pesticides" title="Environmental impact of pesticides">Environmental impact of pesticides</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crop_spraying_near_St_Mary_Bourne_-_geograph.org.uk_-_392462.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Crop_spraying_near_St_Mary_Bourne_-_geograph.org.uk_-_392462.jpg/220px-Crop_spraying_near_St_Mary_Bourne_-_geograph.org.uk_-_392462.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Crop_spraying_near_St_Mary_Bourne_-_geograph.org.uk_-_392462.jpg/330px-Crop_spraying_near_St_Mary_Bourne_-_geograph.org.uk_-_392462.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Crop_spraying_near_St_Mary_Bourne_-_geograph.org.uk_-_392462.jpg/440px-Crop_spraying_near_St_Mary_Bourne_-_geograph.org.uk_-_392462.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crop_spraying_near_St_Mary_Bourne_-_geograph.org.uk_-_392462.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spraying a crop with a <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a></div></div></div>
<p>Pesticide use has increased since 1950 to 2.5 million short tons annually worldwide, yet crop loss from pests has remained relatively constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pimentel_pesticide_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pimentel_pesticide-178">[178]</a></sup> The World Health Organization estimated in 1992 that three million pesticide poisonings occur annually, causing 220,000 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-179">[179]</a></sup> Pesticides select for <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_resistance" title="Pesticide resistance">pesticide resistance</a> in the pest population, leading to a condition termed the "pesticide treadmill" in which pest resistance warrants the development of a new pesticide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_Pest_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-CS_Pest-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>An alternative argument is that the way to "save the environment" and prevent famine is by using pesticides and intensive high yield farming, a view exemplified by a quote heading the Center for Global Food Issues website: 'Growing more per acre leaves more land for nature'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DAvery_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-DAvery-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> However, critics argue that a trade-off between the environment and a need for food is not inevitable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-WH-183">[183]</a></sup> and that pesticides simply replace <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_agricultural_practices" title="Good agricultural practices">good agronomic practices</a> such as crop rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CS_Pest_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-CS_Pest-180">[180]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Push%E2%80%93pull_agricultural_pest_management" title="Push–pull agricultural pest management">Push–pull agricultural pest management</a> technique involves intercropping, using plant aromas to repel pests from crops (push) and to lure them to a place from which they can then be removed (pull).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PushPull_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-PushPull-184">[184]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions_to_climate_change">Contributions to climate change</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture" title="Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture">Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture</a></div>
<p>Agriculture, and in particular animal husbandry, is responsible for <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> of CO<sub>2</sub> and a percentage of the world's methane, and future land infertility, and the displacement of wildlife. Agriculture contributes to climate change by anthropogenic emissions of greenhouse gases, and by the conversion of non-agricultural land such as forest for agricultural use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Agriculture, forestry and land-use change contributed around 20 to 25% to global annual emissions in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> A range of policies can reduce the risk of negative climate change impacts on agriculture,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture" title="Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture">greenhouse gas emissions from the agriculture sector</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability">Sustainability</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TerracesBuffers.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2100" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/TerracesBuffers.JPG/170px-TerracesBuffers.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/TerracesBuffers.JPG/255px-TerracesBuffers.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/TerracesBuffers.JPG/340px-TerracesBuffers.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TerracesBuffers.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Terraces, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_tillage" title="Conservation tillage">conservation tillage</a> and conservation buffers reduce <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a> on this farm in Iowa.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">Sustainable agriculture</a></div>
<p>Current farming methods have resulted in over-stretched water resources, high levels of erosion and reduced soil fertility. There is not enough water to continue farming using current practices; therefore how critical water, land, and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> resources are used to boost crop yields must be reconsidered. A solution would be to give value to ecosystems, recognizing environmental and livelihood tradeoffs, and balancing the rights of a variety of users and interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> Inequities that result when such measures are adopted would need to be addressed, such as the reallocation of water from poor to rich, the clearing of land to make way for more productive farmland, or the preservation of a wetland system that limits fishing rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>Technological advancements help provide farmers with tools and resources to make farming more sustainable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Technology permits innovations like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conservation_tillage" title="Conservation tillage">conservation tillage</a>, a farming process which helps prevent land loss to erosion, reduces water pollution, and enhances <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sequestration" title="Carbon sequestration">carbon sequestration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> Other potential practices include <a href="/wiki/Conservation_agriculture" title="Conservation agriculture">conservation agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agroforestry" title="Agroforestry">agroforestry</a>, improved <a href="/wiki/Convertible_husbandry" title="Convertible husbandry">grazing</a>, avoided grassland conversion, and <a href="/wiki/Biochar" title="Biochar">biochar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Current mono-crop farming practices in the United States preclude widespread adoption of sustainable practices, such as 2-3 crop rotations that incorporate grass or hay with annual crops, unless negative emission goals such as soil carbon sequestration become policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>The International Food Policy Research Institute states that agricultural technologies will have the greatest impact on food production if adopted in combination with each other; using a model that assessed how eleven technologies could impact agricultural productivity, food security and trade by 2050, it found that the number of people at risk from hunger could be reduced by as much as 40% and food prices could be reduced by almost half.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifpri_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-ifpri-138">[138]</a></sup> The food demand of Earth's projected population, with current climate change predictions, could be satisfied by improvement of agricultural methods, expansion of agricultural areas, and a sustainability-oriented consumer mindset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_dependence">Energy dependence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baumwoll-Erntemaschine_auf_Feld.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1805" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Baumwoll-Erntemaschine_auf_Feld.jpeg/220px-Baumwoll-Erntemaschine_auf_Feld.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Baumwoll-Erntemaschine_auf_Feld.jpeg/330px-Baumwoll-Erntemaschine_auf_Feld.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Baumwoll-Erntemaschine_auf_Feld.jpeg/440px-Baumwoll-Erntemaschine_auf_Feld.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baumwoll-Erntemaschine_auf_Feld.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mechanised_agriculture" title="Mechanised agriculture">Mechanised agriculture</a>: from the first models in the 1940s, tools like a <a href="/wiki/Cotton_picker" title="Cotton picker">cotton picker</a> could replace 50 farm workers, at the price of increased use of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Since the 1940s, agricultural productivity has increased dramatically, due largely to the increased use of energy-intensive mechanization, fertilizers and pesticides. The vast majority of this energy input comes from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Between the 1960s and the 1980s, the Green Revolution transformed agriculture around the globe, with world grain production increasing significantly (between 70% and 390% for wheat and 60% to 150% for rice, depending on geographic area)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> as <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> doubled. Heavy reliance on <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemicals</a> has raised concerns that oil shortages could increase costs and reduce agricultural output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncseonline.org_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-ncseonline.org-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p>Industrialized agriculture depends on fossil fuels in two fundamental ways: direct consumption on the farm and manufacture of inputs used on the farm. Direct consumption includes the use of lubricants and fuels to operate farm vehicles and machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncseonline.org_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-ncseonline.org-202">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable floatright">

<p>Indirect consumption includes the manufacture of fertilizers, pesticides, and farm machinery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncseonline.org_202-2"><a href="#cite_note-ncseonline.org-202">[202]</a></sup> In particular, the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fertilizer" title="Nitrogen fertilizer">nitrogen fertilizer</a> can account for over half of agricultural energy usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Together, direct and indirect consumption by US farms accounts for about 2% of the nation's energy use. Direct and indirect energy consumption by U.S. farms peaked in 1979, and has since gradually declined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ncseonline.org_202-3"><a href="#cite_note-ncseonline.org-202">[202]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_systems" title="Food systems">Food systems</a> encompass not just agriculture but off-farm processing, packaging, transporting, marketing, consumption, and disposal of food and food-related items. Agriculture accounts for less than one-fifth of food system energy use in the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ers.usda.gov_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-ers.usda.gov-204">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-css.snre.umich.edu_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-css.snre.umich.edu-207">[207]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plastic_pollution">Plastic pollution</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">Plastic pollution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plasticulture" title="Plasticulture">plasticulture</a></div>
<p>Plastic products are used extensively in agriculture, for example to increase crop yield and improve the efficiency of water and agrichemical use. "Agriplastic" products include films to cover <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse" title="Greenhouse">greenhouses</a> and tunnels, mulch to cover soil (e.g. to suppress <a href="/wiki/Weed" title="Weed">weeds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_conservation" title="Water conservation">conserve water</a>, increase soil temperature and aid fertilizer application), shade cloth, pesticide containers, seedling trays, protective mesh and irrigation tubing. The polymers most commonly used in these products are low- density polyethylene (LPDE), linear low-density polyethylene (LLDPE), polypropylene (PP) and polyvinyl chloride (PVC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>The total amount of plastics used in agriculture is difficult to quantify. A 2012 study reported that almost 6.5 million tonnes per year were consumed globally while a later study estimated that global demand in 2015 was between 7.3 million and 9 million tonnes. Widespread use of plastic mulch and lack of systematic collection and management have led to the generation of large amounts of mulch residue. Weathering and degradation eventually cause the mulch to fragment. These fragments and larger pieces of plastic accumulate in soil. Mulch residue has been measured at levels of 50 to 260 kg per hectare in topsoil in areas where the mulch has been used for more than 10 years, which confirms that mulching is a major source of both microplastic and macroplastic contamination of soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>Agricultural plastics, especially plastic films, are not easy to recycle because of high contamination levels (up to 40- 50% by weight contamination by pesticides, fertilizers, soil and debris, moist vegetation, silage juice water, and UV stabilizers) and collection difficulties . Therefore, they are often buried or abandoned in fields and watercourses or burned. These disposal practices lead to soil degradation and can result in contamination of soils and leakage of microplastics into the marine environment as a result of precipitation run-off and tidal washing. In addition, additives in residual plastic film (such as UV and thermal stabilizers) may have deleterious effects on crop growth, soil structure, nutrient transport and salt levels. There is a risk that plastic mulch will deteriorate soil quality, deplete soil organic matter stocks, increase soil water repellence and emit greenhouse gases. Microplastics released through fragmentation of agricultural plastics can absorb and concentrate contaminants capable of being passed up the trophic chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_208-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disciplines">Disciplines</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_economics">Agricultural economics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_economics" title="Agricultural economics">Agricultural economics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1846_-_Anti-Corn_Law_League_Meeting.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="711" data-file-width="870" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/1846_-_Anti-Corn_Law_League_Meeting.jpg/220px-1846_-_Anti-Corn_Law_League_Meeting.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/1846_-_Anti-Corn_Law_League_Meeting.jpg/330px-1846_-_Anti-Corn_Law_League_Meeting.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/1846_-_Anti-Corn_Law_League_Meeting.jpg/440px-1846_-_Anti-Corn_Law_League_Meeting.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1846_-_Anti-Corn_Law_League_Meeting.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 19th century Britain, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protectionist" title="Protectionist">protectionist</a> <a href="/wiki/Corn_Laws" title="Corn Laws">Corn Laws</a> led to high prices and widespread protest, such as this 1846 meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Corn_Law_League" title="Anti-Corn Law League">Anti-Corn Law League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Agricultural economics is economics as it relates to the "production, distribution and consumption of [agricultural] goods and services".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> Combining agricultural production with general theories of marketing and business as a discipline of study began in the late 1800s, and grew significantly through the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Although the study of agricultural economics is relatively recent, major trends in agriculture have significantly affected national and international economies throughout history, ranging from <a href="/wiki/Tenant_farmer" title="Tenant farmer">tenant farmers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sharecropping" title="Sharecropping">sharecropping</a> in the post-<a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> Southern United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> to the European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feudal" title="Feudal">feudal</a> system of <a href="/wiki/Manorialism" title="Manorialism">manorialism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup> In the United States, and elsewhere, food costs attributed to <a href="/wiki/Food_processing" title="Food processing">food processing</a>, distribution, and <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_marketing" title="Agricultural marketing">agricultural marketing</a>, sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_value_chain" title="Agricultural value chain">value chain</a>, have risen while the costs attributed to farming have declined. This is related to the greater efficiency of farming, combined with the increased level of <a href="/wiki/Value_added" title="Value added">value addition</a> (e.g. more highly processed products) provided by the supply chain. <a href="/wiki/Market_concentration" title="Market concentration">Market concentration</a> has increased in the sector as well, and although the total effect of the increased market concentration is likely increased efficiency, the changes redistribute <a href="/wiki/Economic_surplus" title="Economic surplus">economic surplus</a> from producers (farmers) and consumers, and may have negative implications for rural communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sexton2000_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sexton2000-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>National government policies can significantly change the economic marketplace for agricultural products, in the form of taxation, <a href="/wiki/Subsidy" title="Subsidy">subsidies</a>, tariffs and other measures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LloydCroserAnderson2013_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-LloydCroserAnderson2013-215">[215]</a></sup> Since at least the 1960s, a combination of trade restrictions, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exchange_rate_policy" title="Exchange rate policy">exchange rate policies</a> and subsidies have affected farmers in both the developing and the developed world. In the 1980s, non-subsidized farmers in developing countries experienced adverse effects from national policies that created artificially low global prices for farm products. Between the mid-1980s and the early 2000s, several international agreements limited agricultural tariffs, subsidies and other trade restrictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, as of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Agriculture&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there was still a significant amount of policy-driven distortion in global agricultural product prices. The three agricultural products with the most trade distortion were sugar, milk and rice, mainly due to taxation. Among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oilseed" title="Oilseed">oilseeds</a>, sesame had the most taxation, but overall, feed grains and oilseeds had much lower levels of taxation than livestock products. Since the 1980s, policy-driven distortions have seen a greater decrease among livestock products than crops during the worldwide reforms in agricultural policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LloydCroserAnderson2013_215-1"><a href="#cite_note-LloydCroserAnderson2013-215">[215]</a></sup> Despite this progress, certain crops, such as cotton, still see subsidies in developed countries artificially deflating global prices, causing hardship in developing countries with non-subsidized farmers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup> Unprocessed commodities such as corn, soybeans, and cattle are generally graded to indicate quality, affecting the price the producer receives. Commodities are generally reported by production quantities, such as volume, number or weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_science">Agricultural science</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_science" title="Agricultural science">Agricultural science</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Agronomy" title="Agronomy">Agronomy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Research-mapping_plant_genomes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1235" data-file-width="1407" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Research-mapping_plant_genomes.jpg/220px-Research-mapping_plant_genomes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Research-mapping_plant_genomes.jpg/330px-Research-mapping_plant_genomes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Research-mapping_plant_genomes.jpg/440px-Research-mapping_plant_genomes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Research-mapping_plant_genomes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Agronomy" title="Agronomy">agronomist</a> mapping a plant <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_science" title="Agricultural science">Agricultural science</a> is a broad multidisciplinary field of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> that encompasses the parts of exact, natural, economic and <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social sciences</a> used in the practice and understanding of agriculture. It covers topics such as agronomy, plant breeding and genetics, <a href="/wiki/Plant_pathology" title="Plant pathology">plant pathology</a>, crop modelling, soil science, <a href="/wiki/Entomology" title="Entomology">entomology</a>, production techniques and improvement, study of pests and their management, and study of adverse environmental effects such as soil degradation, <a href="/wiki/Waste_management" title="Waste management">waste management</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bosso_2015_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bosso_2015-219">[219]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boucher_2018_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boucher_2018-220">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>The scientific study of agriculture began in the 18th century, when <a href="/wiki/Johann_Friedrich_Mayer_(agriculturist)" title="Johann Friedrich Mayer (agriculturist)">Johann Friedrich Mayer</a> conducted experiments on the use of <a href="/wiki/Gypsum" title="Gypsum">gypsum</a> (hydrated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcium_sulphate" title="Calcium sulphate">calcium sulphate</a>) as a fertilizer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JB_1840_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-JB_1840-221">[221]</a></sup> Research became more systematic when in 1843, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Lawes" title="John Lawes">John Lawes</a> and Henry Gilbert began a set of long-term agronomy field experiments at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rothamsted_Research_Station" title="Rothamsted Research Station">Rothamsted Research Station</a> in England; some of them, such as the <a href="/wiki/Park_Grass_Experiment" title="Park Grass Experiment">Park Grass Experiment</a>, are still running.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> In America, the <a href="/wiki/Hatch_Act_of_1887" title="Hatch Act of 1887">Hatch Act of 1887</a> provided funding for what it was the first to call "agricultural science", driven by farmers' interest in fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> In agricultural entomology, the USDA began to research biological control in 1881; it instituted its first large program in 1905, searching Europe and Japan for natural enemies of the <a href="/wiki/Lymantria_dispar_dispar" title="Lymantria dispar dispar">gypsy moth</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown-tail" title="Brown-tail">brown-tail</a> moth, establishing <a href="/wiki/Parasitoid" title="Parasitoid">parasitoids</a> (such as solitary wasps) and predators of both pests in the USA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coulson_J._R._2000_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coulson_J._R._2000-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berkeley_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berkeley-226">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy">Policy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_policy" title="Agricultural policy">Agricultural policy</a></div>
<table class="wikitable floatright">
<caption><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_subsidy" title="Agricultural subsidy">Direct subsidies</a> for animal products and feed by <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> countries in 2012, in billions of US dollars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Product</th>
<th>Subsidy
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Beef and veal</td>
<td>18.0
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Milk</td>
<td>15.3
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Pigs</td>
<td>7.3
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Poultry</td>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Soybeans</td>

</td></tr>

<td>Eggs</td>
<td>1.5
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Sheep</td>
<td>1.1
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_policy" title="Agricultural policy">Agricultural policy</a> is the set of government decisions and actions relating to domestic agriculture and imports of foreign agricultural products. Governments usually implement agricultural policies with the goal of achieving a specific outcome in the domestic agricultural product markets. Some overarching themes include risk management and adjustment (including policies related to climate change, food safety and natural disasters), <a href="/wiki/Economic_stability" title="Economic stability">economic stability</a> (including policies related to taxes), natural resources and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_sustainability" title="Environmental sustainability">environmental sustainability</a> (especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_resource_management" title="Water resource management">water policy</a>), research and development, and market access for domestic commodities (including relations with global organizations and agreements with other countries).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Agricultural policy can also touch on <a href="/wiki/Food_quality" title="Food quality">food quality</a>, ensuring that the food supply is of a consistent and known quality, food security, ensuring that the food supply meets the population's needs, and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation</a>. Policy programs can range from financial programs, such as subsidies, to encouraging producers to enroll in voluntary quality assurance programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many influences on the creation of agricultural policy, including consumers, agribusiness, trade lobbies and other groups. <a href="/wiki/Agribusiness" title="Agribusiness">Agribusiness</a> interests hold a large amount of influence over policy making, in the form of <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobbying</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Campaign_contribution" title="Campaign contribution">campaign contributions</a>. Political action groups, including those interested in environmental issues and labor unions, also provide influence, as do lobbying organizations representing individual agricultural commodities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization_of_the_United_Nations" title="Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations">Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations</a> (FAO) leads international efforts to defeat hunger and provides a forum for the negotiation of global agricultural regulations and agreements. Samuel Jutzi, director of FAO's animal production and health division, states that lobbying by large corporations has stopped reforms that would improve human health and the environment. For example, proposals in 2010 for a voluntary code of conduct for the livestock industry that would have provided incentives for improving standards for health, and environmental regulations, such as the number of animals an area of land can support without long-term damage, were successfully defeated due to large food company pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p>
